Method of Learning
beginner
6 min
The Necessity of a Benefit and Definition of Futility
Sanusi explains the necessity of a specific benefit for engaging in a science and defines futility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition for commencing a study of a science: a belief in a specific benefit, and the definition of futility as an action with an insignificant benefit.

Core Idea
Believing in a benefit is necessary; futility is acting for a trivial aim.

This passage from al-Mawaqif sets out the foundational principle that one must have a specific benefit in mind before engaging in a science. The author also defines futility (abath) and addresses a subtle objection about non-specific benefits. Let's walk through his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first few lines where the author states that if one believes a science has a benefit (even if wrong), he may start, but the actual benefit results.
This shows the role of intention.
2
Understand the definition of futility
The author defines futility as an action intended for a benefit that is not regarded as significant. Note that rational people avoid such actions.
This is important for later arguments.
3
Examine the objection about non-specific benefit
An objection is raised: what if one believes in a benefit that is not specific? The answer places it under the case of no specific benefit.
This clarifies the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الأول - المقصد الثالث : فائدته
يتعرض له، وإن اعتقد فيه فائدة غير ما هي فائدته أمكنه الشروع فيه إلا أنه لا يترتب عليه ما اعتقده، بل ما هو فائدته، وربما لم تكن موافقة لغرضه فيعد سعيه في تحصيله
قدحي العطشان وطريقي الهارب، كما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة أمر خفي حتى قال بعض الأذكياء : لا يسلم وجود مثل هذه الصفة لاستلزامه المحال .
قوله : ( وربما لم تكن موافقة لغرضه إنما قال : ربما لأن فائدة العلم إن كانت مباينة لما اعتقده لم تكن مطابقة لغرضه أصلا وإن كانت أعم فربما تتحقق في ضمن الأخص الذي اعتقده، وإن كانت أخص حصل له بعض ما اعتقده وعلى التقديرين تكون موافقة لغرضه، فافهم فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام .
العبث في العرف يطلق على الفعل الذي يقصد به فائدة غير معتد بها وهو الذي يحترز عنه العقلاء ولا شك في كونه متصوراً بل واقعاً .
قوله : ( وإن اعتقد فيه فائدة إلخ قيل ما يقصد فيه فائدة ما غير معينة خارج عن القسمين، ويحتمل ترتب الفائدة المطلوبة، فلا يكون عبثاً مع أنه لم يتصور الفائدة المعينة فالأولى أن يقال : فيه مضاف محذوف تقديره دفعاً لاحتمال العبث، ودفع احتمال الفساد أمر مطلوب عند العقلاء، وقد يجاب أنه داخل في القسم الأول إذ المراد من قوله إن لم يعتقد فيه فائدة أصلاً أن لا يعتقد فائدة معينة، وذا إما بأن لا يعتقد فائدة أصلاً، أو يعتقد فائدةً ما غير معينة، وعلى كلا التقديرين يتصور الشروع، وما وقع في الكتب من أن الموقوف عليه للشروع هو التصور بوجه ما والتصديق بفائدة ما ليس المراد منه التصديق بفائدة غير معينة بل فائدة ،مخصوصة والتعميم بالنظر إلى فائدته في الواقع على أن القول باحتمال ترتب الفائدة المطلوبة في هذه الصورة ليس له كثير معنى كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( وربما لم تكن موافقة لغرضه إن قلت : المفروض أن الشارع في العلم تصور فائدة فائدته في الواقع، ولا شك أن المترتب فائدته الواقعة فعدم موافقتها لغرضه كلي، فلا معنى لرب المفيدة للتقليل أو التكثير قلت: أما أولاً فقد تستعار رب للتحقيق كما ذكره ابن الحاجب في قوله تعالى : وربما يود الذين كفروا لو كانوا مسلمين ﴾ [الحجر: ٢] وأما ثانياً فالمراد بالموافقة الملاءمة لا المطابقة، وحينئذ جاز أن يكون للفائدة المترتبة ملاءمة للفائدة التي اعتقدها الطالب بأن يكون لها نوع تعلق بها، وتوقف عليها وأما ثالثاً فالضمير في عليه من قوله إلا أنه لا يترتب عليه راجع إلى الاعتقاد لا إلى العلم المشروع فيه والمعنى إلا أنه لا يترتب على اعتقاد نفع في شيء ما يقصد بل يترتب ما هو فائدة ذلك الشيء في الواقع فقد تكون موافقة للغرض إذا كان المعتقد فائدته الواقعة، وقد لا تكون إذا لم تكن كذلك، وبالجملة قوله : ربما لم تكن إلخ حكم كلي ليس مقصوراً على المفروض نعم يعلم حال المفروض منه، وقد يقال ليس المراد من الغرض في قوله، وربما لم تكن موافقة لغرضه الغرض من الفعل الذي اعتقد فيه فائدة غير ما هو فائدته في الواقع حتى يتوجه ما ذكر بل المراد غرض الفاعل في الجملة فإنه يجوز أن
The First Station - The Third Purpose: Its Benefit
One engages in it, and if he believes in a benefit other than what its actual benefit is, he can commence it, but what he believes does not result from it, rather what is its actual benefit results, and perhaps it may not be in accordance with his purpose, so his effort in acquiring it would be wasted.
[The example of] the two arrows of the thirsty and the two paths of the fleeing, as the Ash'aris hold, is a subtle matter, to the extent that some intelligent people said: The existence of such an attribute is not conceded because it entails impossibility.
His saying: 'and perhaps it may not be in accordance with his purpose' — He only said 'perhaps' because if the benefit of the science is different from what he believed, then it does not match his purpose at all; if it is more general, then perhaps it may be realized within the more specific that he believed; if it is more specific, then some of what he believed is obtained. In both cases, it is in accordance with his purpose. So understand, for many have stumbled here.
Futility (abath) in conventional usage is applied to an action by which a benefit that is not regarded is intended, and it is that which rational people avoid, and there is no doubt that it is conceivable, indeed actual.
His saying: 'and if he believes in a benefit, etc.' It is said: What is intended by it is a benefit that is not specified, falling outside the two divisions, and the desired benefit may possibly result, so it is not futility, even though he did not conceive a specific benefit. So it is better to say: There is an omitted addition, its implied meaning is 'to repel the possibility of futility', and repelling the possibility of corruption is a desired matter for rational people. And it may be answered that it falls under the first division, because what is meant by his saying 'if he does not believe in a benefit at all' is that he does not believe in a specific benefit. This is either by not believing in any benefit at all, or by believing in some non-specific benefit. In both cases, commencement is conceivable. And what is found in the books that the prerequisite for commencement is conception in some way and assent to some benefit – what is meant is not assent to a non-specific benefit, but a specific benefit, and the generalization is with regard to its actual benefit. Moreover, the claim that the desired benefit could possibly result in this scenario has not much meaning, as is not hidden.
His saying: 'and perhaps it may not be in accordance with his purpose' – If you say: The assumption is that the one engaging in the science has conceived a benefit whose actual benefit is in reality, and there is no doubt that what results is its actual benefit, so its lack of accordance with his purpose is universal, so the word 'perhaps' (which indicates reduction or increase) has no meaning. I say: Firstly, 'perhaps' (رب) may be used for affirmation, as Ibn al-Hajib mentioned in His saying: 'And perhaps those who disbelieve will wish they had been Muslims' [al-Hijr: 2]. Secondly, what is meant by accordance is suitability, not exact matching. In that case, it is possible that the resulting benefit has suitability to the benefit that the student believed, in that it has some relation to it and depends on it. Thirdly, the pronoun in 'upon it' from his saying 'but it does not result upon it' refers to the belief, not to the science that is commenced. The meaning is: It does not result from the belief of benefit in a thing that is intended, rather what results is the actual benefit of that thing. So it may be in accordance with the purpose if what he believed is its actual benefit, and it may not be if that is not the case. In summary, his saying 'perhaps it may not be in accordance' is a universal ruling, not confined to the assumed case. Rather, the case of the assumed is known from it. And it may be said: What is meant by 'purpose' in his saying 'and perhaps it may not be in accordance with his purpose' is not the purpose of the act in which he believed a benefit other than its actual benefit, so as to give rise to what was mentioned, but rather the purpose of the agent in general, for it is possible that
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the necessity of believing in a specific benefit before engaging in a science. It begins by stating that if a person believes a science has a benefit (different from its actual benefit), he may start it, but the actual benefit will result, which may not match his purpose. The author then discusses the subtlety of the Ash'ari position on divine attributes. The main focus is on the word 'perhaps' (ربما): the commentator explains that the author used 'perhaps' because the benefit could be broader, narrower, or the same as believed; in all cases it may partly accord. The definition of futility (abath) is given as an action intended for an insignificant benefit. Then an objection is raised about a non-specific benefit not fitting the two divisions (no benefit vs. specific benefit). The answer places non-specific benefit under 'no benefit' for practical purposes. Another objection concerns the use of 'perhaps' when the benefit is universal; the answer provides three points: (1) 'رب' can affirm, (2) 'accordance' means suitability not exact match, (3) the pronoun 'it' refers to the belief, not the science. The passage concludes that the ruling is general and that the agent's purpose may be unrelated to the specific act.

Key Terms
فائدة
عبث
غرض
ربما

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing in any vague benefit is sufficient to start a science.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only a specific benefit counts; a non-specific benefit falls under the case of no benefit.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of abath (futility) as given in the text?
A) An action with no benefit
B) An action intended for a benefit that is not regarded
C) An action with a non-specific benefit
D) An action that entails impossibility

Key Takeaway
One must believe in a specific benefit before undertaking a science; futility is acting for an insignificant benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sit down to study a new Islamic topic, do you have a clear, specific benefit in mind, or do you just assume it's good? How might that affect your focus and retention?
?
Can you recall a time when you engaged in a religious act without a clear purpose? What was that experience like, and how does this lesson challenge you to change that?
?
If you started learning a science expecting one benefit but discovered the actual benefit was different, would you continue? What does the author's statement 'his effort in acquiring it would be wasted' mean for your own commitment?

Nuances of 'ربما' and the Universal Ruling
The author defends the use of 'ربما' in the original statement with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuances of the word 'ربما' in this context and how the author defends its use through three arguments.

Core Idea
'ربما' is used for affirmation and indicates partial suitability, not uncertainty.

The author now addresses a subtle objection regarding the word 'ربما' (perhaps). The objector claims that if the actual benefit always results, then it can never align with the student's purpose, making 'ربما' meaningless. The author provides a three-part response, clarifying the meaning of 'ربما', the concept of accordance, and the referent of the pronoun.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that since the actual benefit always results, it can never match the student's purpose if he believed a different benefit. So 'ربما' is illogical.
Notice the objection assumes 'ربما' indicates uncertainty.
2
First answer: 'رب' can be used for affirmation
The author cites Ibn al-Hajib's example from the Quran where 'ربما' is used for affirmation, not uncertainty.
This is a linguistic point.
3
Second and third answers
The second answer reinterprets 'accordance' as suitability, not exact match. The third answer shifts the pronoun referent to the belief, not the science.
These show the depth of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الأول - المقصد الثالث : فائدته
يتعرض له، وإن اعتقد فيه فائدة غير ما هي فائدته أمكنه الشروع فيه إلا أنه لا يترتب عليه ما اعتقده، بل ما هو فائدته، وربما لم تكن موافقة لغرضه فيعد سعيه في تحصيله

One engages in it, and if he believes in a benefit other than what its actual benefit is, he can commence it, but what he believes does not result from it, rather what is its actual benefit results, and perhaps it may not be in accordance with his purpose, so his effort in acquiring it would be wasted.

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the necessity of believing in a specific benefit before engaging in a science. It begins by stating that if a person believes a science has a benefit (different from its actual benefit), he may start it, but the actual benefit will result, which may not match his purpose. The author then discusses the subtlety of the Ash'ari position on divine attributes. The main focus is on the word 'perhaps' (ربما): the commentator explains that the author used 'perhaps' because the benefit could be broader, narrower, or the same as believed; in all cases it may partly accord. The definition of futility (abath) is given as an action intended for an insignificant benefit. Then an objection is raised about a non-specific benefit not fitting the two divisions (no benefit vs. specific benefit). The answer places non-specific benefit under 'no benefit' for practical purposes. Another objection concerns the use of 'perhaps' when the benefit is universal; the answer provides three points: (1) 'رب' can affirm, (2) 'accordance' means suitability not exact match, (3) the pronoun 'it' refers to the belief, not the science. The passage concludes that the ruling is general and that the agent's purpose may be unrelated to the specific act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'ربما' always indicates uncertainty or probability.
Correction: The passage shows that 'رب' can be used for affirmation, and in this context it indicates that the accord is not guaranteed but possible.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given for using 'ربما' in the original statement?
A) It indicates uncertainty
B) It is used for affirmation
C) It shows the benefit is unknown
D) It is a rhetorical device

Key Takeaway
The word 'ربما' in this context is not about uncertainty but about the possibility of partial suitability between the believed benefit and the actual benefit, and the pronoun's referent is the belief, not the science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge, do you truly desire the actual benefit of the subject, or are you more attached to your own preconceived ideas of what you will gain from it? How might you realign your intention to seek truth rather than confirmation?
?
Reflect on a time you believed a certain action would bring you benefit, but later realized its actual benefit was different. How does this lesson's distinction between 'موافقة' as suitability versus exact match help you understand that experience? What does it teach you about humility in claiming certainty?
?
The passage mentions that the pronoun 'عليه' refers to belief, not the science itself. What false beliefs have you held about your motives in learning? How can you ensure your pursuit of knowledge is anchored in the reality of Allah's creation and command, rather than your own assumptions?

The Honor of Kalam: Subject, Goal, and Proofs
Al-Iji states the three bases for kalam's honor: its subject, its goal, and its proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold basis for the honor of kalam: its subject (the noblest known), its goal (the highest happiness), and its certain proofs (combining intellect and transmitted evidence).

Core Idea
Kalam is the most honorable science because its subject, goal, and proofs are the noblest.

This passage from al-Mawaqif lays out why ilm al-kalam holds the highest rank among the Islamic sciences. The author, al-Iji, presents three dimensions—subject, goal, and proofs—that together establish kalam's superiority. As you read, notice how each dimension builds on the previous one, culminating in the unique certainty of kalam's arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three dimensions
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Look for the three distinct reasons for kalam's honor.
The words 'معلوم', 'غاية', and 'دلائل' signal the three dimensions.
2
Examine the subject of kalam
The passage says the subject is 'the noblest known' and is qualified by a modality. Consider why studying God's essence, attributes, and acts makes the science noble.
3
Reflect on the goal of kalam
The goal is 'happiness resulting from the five things'. Think about what those five things might be and why that goal is the most beneficial.
The five things likely refer to the five foundational principles of kalam.
4
Understand the uniqueness of the proofs
The proofs are certain because they are judged by pure intellect and supported by transmitted evidence. Contrast this with divine philosophy, which lacks that support.
The phrase 'الغاية في الوثاقة' shows the ultimate reliability.
5
Summarize the lesson
In your own words, state the three honors and how they make kalam the most honorable science.
This solidifies the learning objective.

The First Stand - The Fourth Section: Its Rank. And there is no doubt that when the known is nobler, the knowledge of it is nobler, and its subject is qualified by a modality that indicates its honor also. And its goal, namely that happiness resulting from the five things, is the noblest of goals and the most beneficial. And its proofs are certain, judged by pure intellect without any admixture of illusion, and they are supported by transmitted evidence. And this—the intellect's testimony to their soundness together with their support by transmitted evidence—is the ultimate in reliability, for no doubt remains about the soundness of a proof in which intellect and transmitted evidence agree, unlike the proofs of divine philosophy, for the disagreement of transmitted evidence with them is a testimony that the judgments of their intellects are taken from their illusions, not from their pure intellects, so there is no reliance on them at all. And these mentioned matters concerning honor... by consideration of the honor of the issues, and that is not desired. And fourthly, because his statement 'there is no doubt that when the known is nobler' etc. proves the honor of knowledge by consideration of its known, while the intent is to prove its honor by consideration of its subject. And the aim of his direction—may his secret be sanctified—is that he interpreted 'higher' in the sense of 'nobler', not 'higher in rank', so that it would be an establishment. And the 'fa' in his statement 'so it encompasses' is causative, or resumptive, or redundant, and the sentence is a cause for its being the noblest of subjects. And the implicit pronoun refers to its subject, and the restriction of modality is considered, meaning that it encompasses its subject insofar as it is a subject, i.e., investigated in the science, the noblest of known things which are His essence, attributes, and acts, insofar as they are investigated. And he was not satisfied with its encompassing the three things in themselves because that does not entail the honor of the science. Do you not see that the subject of grammar encompasses the speech of God and the speech of the Messenger, but that does not entail its being nobler than exegesis and hadith? And to indicate that the encompassing from the aspect of investigation is the locus of honor, he placed the word 'investigations' first. So the conclusion is that the subject of kalam is the most general of subjects, thus it is nobler, because sciences ascend with the ascent of their subjects, and that its subject is the noblest of subjects because it encompasses His essence, attributes, and acts, which are the noblest of known things. And there is no doubt that when the known which is the subject is nobler due to that encompassing, the knowledge pertaining to it, i.e., investigating its states, is nobler. And its summary is the proof of the honor of its subject by virtue of its inclusivity of subjects and by virtue of itself. Therefore he expressed the subject in his statement 'there is no doubt' etc. as 'the known'. His statement: 'beneficial' is a specification of the relative noun 'the most beneficial', and it is a superlative form from 'jada yajdu jadwa' meaning giving, and it is not a direct object because the superlative does not operate on an explicit direct object. His statement: 'disagreement of transmitted evidence' means definitive, because conjectural transmitted evidence that contradicts definitive intellect is interpreted in a way that agrees with it. His statement: 'and its proofs are certain etc.' It was objected: it has already been mentioned that the issues of those who err in kalam are such; how then are its proofs certain? And it was answered: by specification. His statement: 'judged by pure intellect' i.e., its purest form. In the Sihah, 'sarih' means pure of everything, and 'saraha' in the passive means to be clear. So his statement 'without any admixture of illusion' indicates that what is meant is pure intellect.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the fourth section regarding the honor (sharaf) of ilm al-kalam. It begins by stating that if the known (al-ma'lum) is nobler, then knowledge of it is nobler. The subject of kalam is qualified by a modality (haythiyya) indicating its honor. The goal of kalam—the happiness resulting from the five matters (probably the five pillars or categories)—is the noblest and most beneficial of goals. Its proofs are certain, judged by pure intellect without any illusion, and are supported by transmitted evidence (naql). The combination of intellect and transmitted evidence gives the highest reliability. The passage contrasts this with divine philosophy (al-ilm al-ilahi), whose proofs are unreliable because they contradict transmitted evidence, showing that their intellects are tainted by illusion. The commentary then discusses the word 'higher' (a'la) and explains that 'higher' here means 'nobler' (ashraf) to establish a new point. The 'fa' in 'fayatanawalu' is analyzed as causative or resumptive. The subject of kalam is the most general of subjects, encompassing God's essence, attributes, and acts, which are the noblest known things. This generality makes kalam nobler than other sciences, as sciences ascend with their subjects. The passage then addresses an objection: if some issues in kalam are erroneous, how can its proofs be certain? The answer is by specification (takhsis)—only certain proofs are certain. Finally, 'pure intellect' (sarih al-aql) is explained as intellect free from illusion, as in the Sihah dictionary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam's honor comes only from its subject matter.
Correction: Kalam's honor is based on three things: its subject (the noblest known), its goal (the highest happiness), and its certain proofs (combining intellect and transmission).

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Iji says that kalam's proofs leave no doubt because intellect and transmitted evidence agree. Consider a theological question you have struggled with. Have you been relying on one to the exclusion of the other? What would it look like for you to seek both pure intellectual inquiry and transmitted guidance for that issue? Commit to identifying one such question and spend time with both sources this week.
?

Are you studying kalam primarily as a means to that end, or have you turned it into a mere mental exercise? How can you realign your intention today so that your learning brings you closer to that happiness?

The Honor of Kalam and the Need to Preview Its Issues
Establishes the honor of kalam and the necessity of issue overview.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam is considered the most honorable science and why it is necessary to give students a general overview of its issues before delving into detailed study.

Core Idea
Kalam's honor lies in its subject, goal, and proof; previewing issues sharpens the student's insight.

The author begins by establishing the foundational honor of kalam. He points out that the nobility of any science is derived from its known subject, its goal, and its proofs. Kalam excels in all three. He then stresses the pedagogical importance of giving students a preliminary overview of the issues they will encounter, so that they approach the subject with clear direction and increased insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice how the author defines the honor of kalam in terms of three dimensions.
The passage is dense; focus on the main claims first.
2
Identify the three dimensions of honor
The passage states that the honor of a science rests on its known (subject), its goal, and its proof. Find these in the text.
These correspond to 'معلومه', 'غايته', and 'حجته'.
3
Understand the need for issue preview
The author explains why it is necessary to give a general indication of the issues before starting. Reflect on how this helps the student.
The key phrase is 'ليتنبه الطالب على ما يتوجه إليه من المطالب تنبهاً موجباً لمزيد استبصاره'.
4
Connect to broader methodology
Consider how this preview fits into the overall structure of a kalam text. It is a common pedagogical technique.

The First Observatory - The Fifth Section: Its Issues Which Are the Goals. Ilm al-kalam, meaning its known, its goal, and its proof, are the dimensions of the honor of a science that do not exceed them, i.e., the dimensions of honor do not go beyond these matters we have mentioned. As for the issues of a science being more upright, that refers to the excellence and reliability of the proofs. Thus kalam is then the most honorable of sciences according to all dimensions of honor. The Fifth Section: Its Issues Which Are the Goals. Without the word 'fi' and that is appropriate for what preceded and what followed, and what is found in many copies is 'in its issues'. And it was necessary to give a general indication of the issues of the science which one intends to begin so that the student pays attention to the goals he is heading towards, an attention that causes increased insight in seeking them. And he only said: 'which are the goals' because every codified science has issues that are its original goals, and they are its reality and its principles, either conceptual or assertive, which are means to those goals, and they are sometimes considered a part of it due to the intense need for them. As for counting its subject as a third part of it, that is because the subject itself is among the conceptual principles, and its being a subject for it is among the premises... His statement: 'in its issues' means an explanation of the obligation to present the issues of every science that is begun generally, so that it agrees with what preceded. And it is permissible that its meaning is an explanation of the issues of kalam in general, and it is an explanation of the obligation to present the issues of every science with its proof that the commentator mentioned implicitly in the discourse, for the mind's flow to it after observing what preceded. Then the pronoun 'hiya' refers to his statement 'its issues', except that the commentator—may God have mercy on him—made the pronoun of 'its issues' refer to the science that is intended to be begun, and the proof is left due to its obviousness, and he made the pronoun 'hiya' refer to what is understood from the context, namely the issues of kalam, in observance of conformity with what preceded and to minimize elision. His statement: 'and he only said: etc.' means the restriction by the attribute is not to exclude issues that are not goals, but rather to alert by limiting the goals to them as being intended essentially and that they are the reality of the science and the principles are means. His statement: 'that' the subject, i.e., the subject of the science, not the concept of subject in absolute terms, for it is explained in the art of demonstration from logic that it is not specific to any science, so it is appropriate to mention it in logic, which is a tool for all sciences. His statement: 'from the conceptual principles' because it occurs as the subject of an issue. And what was said that it is a premise for the premise of the enterprise because the belief in subjecthood depends on it, so how is it counted among the principles? So in that is that its being among the principles of the enterprise does not negate its being among the principles of the sciences. It was said: the subject itself, although it is among the conceptual principles, but because it is distinguished from others by being the reason why the numerous issues become one science, the issues have more connection to it and need for it, so it deserves to be taken out of the principles and to be counted...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the fifth section, titled 'Its Issues Which Are the Goals'. It begins by summarizing that the honor of kalam lies in its known (subject), goal, and proof, and that these are the dimensions of honor. Then it states that the issues of a science being 'more upright' relates to the excellence of proofs, and that kalam is the most honorable science in all respects. The commentary then addresses why the phrase 'in its issues' appears in some manuscript versions, and discusses the necessity of giving a general introduction to the issues of a science before beginning, so the student gains insight. The phrase 'which are the goals' indicates that every science has issues that are its original goals (al-maqasid al-asliyya), while its principles (mabadi) are means to those goals. Some consider the principles as part of the science due to need. The passage discusses whether the subject (mawdu') should be considered a third part of the science. It argues that the subject itself is among the conceptual principles (al-mabadi al-tasawwuriyya), and its being a subject is a premise. The commentary then explains the interpretation of the pronoun 'hiya' and the phrase 'in its issues', covering different readings. Finally, it discusses that the subject is distinguished from other principles because it unites the numerous issues into one science, giving it a special status that may warrant separating it from the principles.

The Structure of a Science: Goals, Principles, and the Subject Debate
Explains the goals vs. principles structure and the subject's place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the issues (masail) as the goals of a science and its principles (mabadi), and why the subject (mawdu') is considered part of the conceptual principles rather than a separate component.

Core Idea
Issues are the real goals of a science; principles are means, and the subject belongs to the conceptual principles.

Having established the honor of kalam, the author now delves into the internal structure of a science. He distinguishes between the issues (masail), which are the primary goals (maqasid), and the principles (mabadi), which serve as means to reach those goals. The commentary then tackles a subtle debate: whether the subject of the science (mawdu') should be counted as a third component alongside issues and principles, or whether it is already included within the conceptual principles. The passage argues for the latter view, providing a nuanced justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key parts
Read the Arabic passage, noting the definitions of 'issues' and 'principles'. Also find the argument about the subject.
The phrase 'التي هي المقاصد' is central.
2
Understand the goals vs. means relationship
The passage says that every science has issues that are its original goals, and principles (conceptual or assertive) are means to those goals. Sometimes principles are considered part of the science due to need.
Distinguish: means (mabadi) vs. ends (masail).
3
Analyze the subject debate
Some count the subject as a third part. The passage argues that the subject itself is from the conceptual principles. Understand the reasoning: the subject unites the issues into one science, so it has a special status but is still a principle.
The key phrase: 'الموضوع نفسه من المبادي التصورية'.
4
Reflect on the implications for kalam
Consider how this structural understanding affects the way we study kalam. The subject of kalam is God's essence and attributes; it is a principle that shapes all issues.

علم الكلام أعني معلومه وغايته وحجته ( هي جهات شرف العلم لا تعدوها ) أي لا تتجاوز جهات الشرف هذه الأمور التي ذكرناها ، وأما كون مسائل العلم ،أقوم، فراجع إلى فضيلة الدلائل ووثاقتها ( فهو ) فالكلام (إذا أشرف العلوم بحسب جميع جهات الشرف .
المقصد الخامس : مسائله التي هي المقاصد ]
بدون كلمة في وهو المناسب لما تقدم وما تأخر والموجود في كثير من النسخ في مسائله وإنما وجب تقديم الإشارة الإجمالية إلى مسائل العلم الذي يطلب الشروع فيه ليتنبه الطالب على ما يتوجه إليه من المطالب تنبهاً موجباً لمزيد استبصاره في طلبها وإنما قال : ( التي هي المقاصد ) لأن كل علم مدون له مسائل المقاصد الأصلية فيه، وهي حقيقته ومباد إما تصورية أو تصديقية هي وسائل إلى تلك المقاصد ، وربما عدت جزءاً منه لشدة الحاجة إليها ، وأما عد ،موضوعه جزءاً ثالثاً منه ففيه أن الموضوع نفسه من المبادي التصورية وكونه موضوعاً له من مقدمات
هي
قوله: (في مسائله أي بيان وجوب تقديم مسائل كل علم شرع فيه إجمالاً ليكون موافقاً لما تقدم ويجوز أن يكون معناه في بيان مسائل الكلام إجمالاً، ويكون بيان وجوب تقديم مسائل كل علم مع دليله الذي ذكره الشارح مطوياً في الكلام لانسياق الذهن إليه، بعد ملاحظة ما تقدم، وحينئذ يكون ضمير هي راجعاً إلى قوله مسائله إلا أن الشارح رحمه الله، جعل ضمير مسائله راجعاً إلى العلم الذي يطلب الشروع فيه، والدليل متروكاً لظهوره وجعل ضمير هي راجعاً إلى ما يفهم من السياق أعني مسائل الكلام رعاية للمطابقة بما تقدم وتقليلاً للحذف . قوله : ( وإنما قال: إلخ) يعني ليس التقييد بالصفة للاحتراز عن المسائل التي ليست بمقاصد بل للتنبيه بحصر المقاصد عليها على أنها المقصودة بالذات وإنها حقيقة العلم والمبادي وسائل.
قوله : (أن) الموضوع أي موضوع العلم لا مفهوم الموضوع مطلقاً فإنه يبين في صناعة البرهان من المنطق أن لا اختصاص له بشيء من العلوم فيناسب إيراده في المنطق الذي هو
لجميع العلوم.
آلة
قوله: (من المبادي (التصورية لوقوعه موضوع المسألة وما قيل إنه مقدمة لمقدمة المشروع لتوقف التصديق بالموضوعية عليه، فكيف يعد من المبادي؟ ففيه أن كونه من مبادي الشروع لا ينافي كونه من مبادي العلوم قيل إن الموضوع نفسه، وإن كان من المبادي التصورية
قوله : ففيه أن الموضوع نفسه من المبادي التصورية يمكن أن يقال : الموضوع نفسه، وإن كان من المبادي التصورية لكن لما امتاز عن غيره بكونه سبباً لأن تعد المسائل المتكثرة علماً واحداً كان للمسائل مزید ارتباط به واحتياج إليه فاستحق لأن يخرج من المبادي، ويعد
The First Observatory - The Fifth Section: Its Issues Which Are the Goals. Ilm al-kalam, meaning its known, its goal, and its proof, are the dimensions of the honor of a science that do not exceed them, i.e., the dimensions of honor do not go beyond these matters we have mentioned. As for the issues of a science being more upright, that refers to the excellence and reliability of the proofs. Thus kalam is then the most honorable of sciences according to all dimensions of honor. The Fifth Section: Its Issues Which Are the Goals. Without the word 'fi' and that is appropriate for what preceded and what followed, and what is found in many copies is 'in its issues'. And it was necessary to give a general indication of the issues of the science which one intends to begin so that the student pays attention to the goals he is heading towards, an attention that causes increased insight in seeking them. And he only said: 'which are the goals' because every codified science has issues that are its original goals, and they are its reality and its principles, either conceptual or assertive, which are means to those goals, and they are sometimes considered a part of it due to the intense need for them. As for counting its subject as a third part of it, that is because the subject itself is among the conceptual principles, and its being a subject for it is among the premises... His statement: 'in its issues' means an explanation of the obligation to present the issues of every science that is begun generally, so that it agrees with what preceded. And it is permissible that its meaning is an explanation of the issues of kalam in general, and it is an explanation of the obligation to present the issues of every science with its proof that the commentator mentioned implicitly in the discourse, for the mind's flow to it after observing what preceded. Then the pronoun 'hiya' refers to his statement 'its issues', except that the commentator—may God have mercy on him—made the pronoun of 'its issues' refer to the science that is intended to be begun, and the proof is left due to its obviousness, and he made the pronoun 'hiya' refer to what is understood from the context, namely the issues of kalam, in observance of conformity with what preceded and to minimize elision. His statement: 'and he only said: etc.' means the restriction by the attribute is not to exclude issues that are not goals, but rather to alert by limiting the goals to them as being intended essentially and that they are the reality of the science and the principles are means. His statement: 'that' the subject, i.e., the subject of the science, not the concept of subject in absolute terms, for it is explained in the art of demonstration from logic that it is not specific to any science, so it is appropriate to mention it in logic, which is a tool for all sciences. His statement: 'from the conceptual principles' because it occurs as the subject of an issue. And what was said that it is a premise for the premise of the enterprise because the belief in subjecthood depends on it, so how is it counted among the principles? So in that is that its being among the principles of the enterprise does not negate its being among the principles of the sciences. It was said: the subject itself, although it is among the conceptual principles, but because it is distinguished from others by being the reason why the numerous issues become one science, the issues have more connection to it and need for it, so it deserves to be taken out of the principles and to be counted...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the fifth section, titled 'Its Issues Which Are the Goals'. It begins by summarizing that the honor of kalam lies in its known (subject), goal, and proof, and that these are the dimensions of honor. Then it states that the issues of a science being 'more upright' relates to the excellence of proofs, and that kalam is the most honorable science in all respects. The commentary then addresses why the phrase 'in its issues' appears in some manuscript versions, and discusses the necessity of giving a general introduction to the issues of a science before beginning, so the student gains insight. The phrase 'which are the goals' indicates that every science has issues that are its original goals (al-maqasid al-asliyya), while its principles (mabadi) are means to those goals. Some consider the principles as part of the science due to need. The passage discusses whether the subject (mawdu') should be considered a third part of the science. It argues that the subject itself is among the conceptual principles (al-mabadi al-tasawwuriyya), and its being a subject is a premise. The commentary then explains the interpretation of the pronoun 'hiya' and the phrase 'in its issues', covering different readings. Finally, it discusses that the subject is distinguished from other principles because it unites the numerous issues into one science, giving it a special status that may warrant separating it from the principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the subject of a science is a separate component independent from the principles.
Correction: The passage argues that the subject itself is among the conceptual principles, not an independent third part.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why should the subject (mawdu') of a science not be counted as a third part separate from the principles?
A) Because the subject is not needed for the science.
B) Because the subject itself is among the conceptual principles.
C) Because the subject is same as the goal.
D) Because the principles are more important.

Key Takeaway
A science's issues are its real goals, principles are means, and the subject is a conceptual principle that unifies those issues into a single discipline.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the issues are the real goals of a science and the principles are just means. In your own study of 'Aqeedah, are you more focused on mastering the principles (definitions, classifications) or on internalizing the actual issues about Allah, faith, and the unseen? What would change if you shifted your priority to the goals?
?
The subject of a science makes many issues into one unified discipline. What is the central subject that holds together everything you believe about Aqeedah? Is your understanding of various topics (Tawheed, prophethood, the afterlife) truly united around that subject, or do they feel disconnected? How can you strengthen that unity?
?
The passage warns that the intense need for principles sometimes makes them counted as part of the science itself. Have you ever valued the tools of learning (like memorizing terms or following a methodology) more than the actual knowledge of Allah they are supposed to serve? Ask yourself: what is one principle you have treated as an end in itself, and how will you reorient it back to being a means?

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
Why Present Issues Before Studying a Science?
Author explains necessity of general overview and clarifies issues vs. principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological reason for giving a general overview of a science's issues before delving into detailed study, and the distinction between issues (objectives) and principles (means).

Core Idea
A map of the destination makes the journey clearer.

Before diving into any science, it helps to know what its core issues are. The author explains that this overview awakens the student's attention and sharpens insight. He also clarifies a key distinction: the issues themselves are the real objectives, while the principles (conceptual and assertive) are merely means to those objectives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice the textual variant mentioned at the beginning.
The variant is about the presence or absence of the word 'في'.
2
Identify the main claim
What is the author's main argument? He says it is necessary to give a general indication of the issues before starting. Find the reason he gives.
Look for the phrase 'ليتنبه الطالب'.
3
Distinguish issues from principles
The author clarifies that issues ('مسائل') are the objectives ('مقاصد'), while principles ('مبادئ') are means. He divides principles into two types: conceptual and assertive. Notice how he says these are sometimes counted as part of the science.
The two types are 'تصورية' (conceptual) and 'تصديقية' (assertive).
4
Reflect on the pedagogical benefit
Consider why this overview is beneficial. The text says it alerts the student and increases insight. How might this help in learning a new field?
Observation: Studying with a roadmap prevents confusion about the direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : مسائله التي هي المقاصد ] بدون كلمة في وهو المناسب لما تقدم وما تأخر والموجود في كثير من النسخ في مسائله وإنما وجب تقديم الإشارة الإجمالية إلى مسائل العلم الذي يطلب الشروع فيه ليتنبه الطالب على ما يتوجه إليه من المطالب تنبهاً موجباً لمزيد استبصاره في طلبها وإنما قال : ( التي هي المقاصد ) لأن كل علم مدون له مسائل المقاصد الأصلية فيه، وهي حقيقته ومباد إما تصورية أو تصديقية هي وسائل إلى تلك المقاصد ، وربما عدت جزءاً منه لشدة الحاجة إليها
The fifth objective: its issues which are the objectives. Without the letter 'fi' which is appropriate for what came before and after, and it is found in many copies as 'fi masailihi'. And it was necessary to present a general indication of the issues of the science which one intends to begin, so that the student becomes alerted to what he is directed towards of demands, an alert that necessitates an increase in his insight in seeking them. And he only said 'which are the objectives' because every codified science has its original objective issues, which are its reality and principles, either conceptual or assertive, which are means to those objectives, and perhaps they are counted as part of it due to the intense need for them.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage explains the methodological principle that a science should be prefaced with a general overview of its issues. The author begins by noting a textual variant (the presence or absence of the word 'في') and then states the reason: to alert the student and enhance his insight. He then clarifies that the term 'issues which are the objectives' refers to the core reality of the science and its principles (both conceptual and assertive) that serve as means to those objectives. These means are sometimes considered integral to the science due to the pressing need for them.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the principles of a science are the main objectives, not the issues.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the issues are the real objectives, while principles (whether conceptual or assertive) are merely means to achieve those objectives, even if they are often counted as part of the science.

Checkpoint
Why is it beneficial to present a general overview of the issues of a science before studying it?
A) To satisfy the requirements of the curriculum
B) To alert the student and increase his insight
C) To prove the existence of the subject
D) To distinguish between conceptual and assertive principles

Key Takeaway
Previewing the issues of a science sharpens the student's focus, and understanding that issues are the objectives while principles are their means is essential for proper study.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one Islamic science you are currently studying or have studied. Did you begin with a clear map of its main issues and objectives, or did you dive straight into details? How has that choice shaped your understanding and motivation? Will you change your approach?
?
Are you treating the principles you learn (like grammar rules, hadith terminology, or usul concepts) as ends in themselves, or as means to grasp the real issues of the science? What would it look like to prioritize the objectives this week?
?
When you next start a new chapter or book, will you intentionally seek an overview of its issues first? What specific step will you take to alert your heart and sharpen your insight before diving into details?

Method of Learning
intermediate
7 min
The Subject of a Science as a Conceptual Principle
The author defines the subject of a science and defends its status as a conceptual principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject of a science is considered a conceptual principle and how the objection regarding it being a premise for commencement is resolved.

Core Idea
The subject of a science is a conceptual principle, not merely a premise.

The author begins by clarifying that the subject of a science is not the absolute subject discussed in logic. He then addresses an objection: if the conception of the subject is a premise for the premise of commencing the science, how can it be a principle? This lesson walks through the debate and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of 'subject'
Read the first sentence: the subject of a science is distinct from the absolute concept of subject. The latter belongs to logic.
This distinction is crucial to avoid confusion.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that since the subject's conception is required for the assent to its subject-ness, it is a premise for the premise of commencement, and thus not a principle.
3
Follow the reply
The author's reply: being a principle of commencement does not negate being a principle of the science itself. This shows the distinction between different senses of 'principle'.
The subject is a principle in the sense of being the foundation of the science's issues.
4
Examine the additional argument
The passage then adds that the subject itself is a conceptual principle, while its existence is an assertive principle. Notice this preview of the next lesson.
This sets up the next discussion on existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فإنه) أن الموضوع أي موضوع العلم لا مفهوم الموضوع مطلقاً فإنه يبين في صناعة البرهان من المنطق أن لا اختصاص له بشيء من العلوم فيناسب إيراده في المنطق الذي هو آلة لجميع العلوم. قوله: (من المبادي التصورية لوقوعه موضوع المسألة وما قيل إنه مقدمة لمقدمة المشروع لتوقف التصديق بالموضوعية عليه، فكيف يعد من المبادي؟ ففيه أن كونه من مبادي الشروع لا ينافي كونه من مبادي العلوم قيل إن الموضوع نفسه، وإن كان من المبادي التصورية الشروع فيه الخارجة عنه ،اتفاقا وأنيته أعني وجوده من المبادي التصديقية المسماة عندهم أصولاً موضـوعـة كما صرح به ابن سينا في برهان الشفاء ( وهي ) أي مسائل إلا أنه عد مطلوباً برأسه لشدة ارتباط المسائل به وفيه أنه ينافي ما قالوا في تعليله بأن ما لا يعلم ثبوته، كيف يطلب ثبوت شيء له؟ فإنه صريح بأن المراد به وجوده . قوله : (الخارجة عنه (اتفاقاً وذلك لأن التصديق بموضوعية الموضوع بعد صيرورته موضوعاً، وهي بعد البحث عن عوارضه الذاتية فكيف يكون جزءاً من العلم. قوله : (أعني وجوده من المبادي التصديقية إلخ) أي إن كان خفي الوجود صرح به في الشفاء حيث قال : وموضوع الصناعة فقد يجب أن يصدق به، وأن يتصور جميعاً فما كان ظاهر الوجود خفي الحد مثل الجسم الطبيعي لم يوضع وجوده في العلم بل اشتغل بأن يوضع حده فقط وما كان خفي الوجود والحد معاً مثل العدد والواحد والنقطة فإنهم يضعون وجوده أيضاً، ووضع وجوده من جملة مبادي الصناعة التي تسمى أصولاً موضوعة لأنه مقدمة مشكوك فيها يبتنى عليها الصناعة انتهى بقي أنه قال : في فصل سابق على هذا الفصل أن لكل واحد من الصناعة، وخصوصاً النظرية مبادي وموضوعات ومسائل والمبادي هي المقدمات التي منها يبرهن على تلك الصناعة، وقال : أيضاً فيه المبادي الخاصة لمسائل علم ما على قسمين إما أن تكون خاصة بحسب ذلك العلم كله أو بحسب مسألة أو مسائل انتهى ويعلم من كلاميه أنه قد يطلق المبادي على ما يبتني عليه الصناعة مطلقاً، وقد يطلق على المقدمات التي يبرهن منها على تلك الصناعة، فيمكن أن يكون عد إنية موضوعة جزءاً ثالثاً نظراً إلى المعنى الثاني الذي باعتباره جعل المبادي جزءاً من العلم، وإن كان داخلاً في المبادي بالمعنى الأول، وإليه يشير كلام العلامة التفتازاني في شرح المقاصد ، وفي توصيف الشارح رحمه الله المبادي التصديقية بقوله التي تسمى أصولاً موضوعة رد على القول بأن الشيخ عده من المبادي التصديقية بالمعنى اللغوي لا بالمعنى المصطلح . جزءاً برأسه بقي ها هنا بحث وهو أن المشهور فيما بينهم هو أن المبادي ما يتوقف عليه ذات المسألة، والمقدمة ما يتوقف عليه الشروع وتصور الموضوع ما كان موقوفاً عليه التصديق بموضوعيته، وهو من مقدمات الشروع اتفاقاً كان تصوره مقدمة المقدمة فكيف يعد من المبادي؟ اللهم إلا أن يقال : المراد بالمبادي ها هنا ما يتوقف عليه المسألة ذاتاً وشروعاً لا المبادي المصطلح عليها وفيه ما فيه . قوله : ( وأنيته) أعني وجوده من المبادي التصديقية قد يقال : المبادي التصديقية المصطلح عليها عندهم هي المقدمات التي يتألف منها قياسات العلم، والتصديق بوجود الموضوع ليس منها، وأما تصريح ابن سينا بأن التصديق بالوجود من المبادي التصديقية، فأراد به المعنى اللغوي من حيث أن إثبات الأعراض الذاتية للموضوع يتوقف عليه، ويرد عليه أن بعض المبادي التصديقية يتوقف عليه صحة الدليل، ولا يتركب منه ولا تنحصر حينئذ أجزاء العلوم في الثلاثة نعم في عدانية الموضوع من الأصول الموضوعة تأمل لأنها القضايا التي يتألف منها
His saying: (for it) that the subject, i.e., the subject of the science, not the concept of the subject absolutely, for it is explained in the art of demonstration from logic that it does not belong specifically to any of the sciences, so it is appropriate to mention it in logic which is the tool for all sciences. His saying: (from the conceptual principles) because it serves as the subject of the issue; and what was said that it is a premise for the premise of the commencement because the assent to its subject-ness depends on it, so how is it counted among the principles? So in it (the reply) that its being from the principles of commencement does not negate its being from the principles of the sciences. It was said: the subject itself, even if it is among the conceptual principles... (continues)... his saying: (and its thatness) i.e., its existence, from the assertive principles called among them 'postulated principles' as Ibn Sina stated in his Burhan al-Shifa'. (They) i.e., issues, except that he counted it as a separate sought thing due to the intense connection of issues to it; and in it that it contradicts what they said in their argument that what does not know its existence, how can one seek a thing for it? For it is clear that what is meant is its existence. His saying: (outside of it) by agreement, and that is because the assent to the subject-ness of the subject after it becomes a subject, and it is after the investigation of its essential accidents, so how can it be part of the science? His saying: (I mean its existence from the assertive principles etc.) i.e., if its existence is hidden, he clarified it in al-Shifa where he said: 'The subject of an art must be assented to and conceived together. So what is manifest in existence but hidden in its definition, like the natural body, its existence is not posited in the science; rather, they occupy themselves with positing its definition only. And what is hidden in both existence and definition, like number, the one, and the point, they posit its existence as well. And positing its existence is among the principles of the art called 'postulated principles' because it is a doubted premise upon which the art is built.' End. He also said in a preceding chapter: 'For each of the arts, especially the theoretical, there are principles, subjects, and issues. The principles are the premises from which proofs are derived for that art.' He also said: 'The specific principles of the issues of a science are of two divisions: either specific to the whole science or to an issue or issues.' End. And from his two statements it is known that 'principles' may be applied to what the art is built upon absolutely, and may be applied to the premises from which proofs are derived for that art. So it is possible that counting the thatness of the subject as a third part is with respect to the second meaning, by which the principles are made a part of the science, even if it falls under the principles in the first meaning. And to this refers the statement of al-Allama al-Taftazani in Sharh al-Maqasid. And in the description of the commentator (may God have mercy on him) of the assertive principles by saying 'which are called postulated principles' is a refutation of the statement that the Shaykh counted it among the assertive principles in the linguistic sense, not the technical sense. As a separate part, there remains here a discussion: the well-known among them is that the principles are what the very issue depends on, and the premise is what the commencement depends on. The conception of the subject was something upon which the assent to its subject-ness depends, and it is from the premises of commencement by agreement; its conception was a premise of the premise, so how can it be counted among the principles? Unless it is said that what is meant by principles here is what the issue depends on in terms of essence and commencement, not the technical principles, and there is something in it. His saying: (and its thatness) i.e., its existence, from the assertive principles. It may be said: The technical assertive principles according to them are the premises that constitute the syllogisms of the science, and the assent to the existence of the subject is not from them. As for Ibn Sina's statement that assent to existence is from the assertive principles, he meant by it the linguistic sense, in that proving the essential accidents of the subject depends on it. And it is objected to him that some assertive principles, the validity of the proof depends on them, and they are not composed from them; and then the parts of the sciences are not confined to three. Yes, in counting the thatness of the subject as a postulated principle, there is consideration because they are the propositions that constitute...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a detailed critical discussion about the status of the subject (الموضوع) of a science and its existence (الإنية) in relation to the principles of that science. The author begins by examining the nature of the subject itself: it is the subject of the science, not the absolute concept of subject, which is discussed in logic. He then addresses the objection that if the subject is among the conceptual principles, it cannot be a principle because it is a premise for the premise of commencement; the response is that being a principle of commencement does not exclude it from being a principle of the science. Next, the discussion moves to the existence of the subject. Ibn Sina is cited as including the existence of the subject among the assertive principles, called 'postulated principles'. The author notes that this is particularly for subjects whose existence is not obvious. He distinguishes between two meanings of 'principles': (1) that upon which the art is built absolutely, and (2) the premises from which proofs are derived. Counting the existence as a third part (subject, principles, issues) may be based on the second meaning. The passage includes further debate: the well-known view distinguishes between principles (what the issue itself depends on) and premises (what commencement depends on). Conception of the subject is a premise for commencement, so it cannot be a principle in the technical sense unless one adopts a broader definition. Finally, the author objects that Ibn Sina's inclusion of existence as a principle may be linguistic, not technical, and notes that if existence were a principle, then the parts of the sciences would not be limited to three.

Key Terms
موضوع
مبادي تصورية
مقدمات الشروع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the subject of a science is the same as the absolute subject of logic.
Correction: The subject of a science is specific; the absolute subject is a logical concept used across sciences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reply to the objection that the subject's conception is a premise for the premise of commencement?
A) It is not a principle at all
B) Being a principle of commencement does not negate being a principle of the science
C) It is only a conceptual principle, not a real one
D) The objection is invalid because the subject is not a premise

Key Takeaway
The subject of a science is a conceptual principle, and being a premise for commencement does not remove it from being a principle of the science.

The Existence of the Subject as an Assertive Principle
The author discusses Ibn Sina's view on the subject's existence as a principle and the ensuing debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's classification of the subject's existence as an assertive principle and the debate over the meaning of 'principles' in the sciences.

Core Idea
The existence of the subject is an assertive principle when it is hidden, but its status is debated.

The passage moves to the existence (thatness) of the subject. Ibn Sina is cited for including it among the assertive principles, especially for subjects with hidden existence. However, the author discusses the two meanings of 'principles' and presents objections that challenge this inclusion. This lesson unpacks these nuanced discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's classification
Read the quote from al-Shifa: for subjects with hidden existence and definition, existence is posited as a principle.
This is the core evidence for the claim.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of 'principles'
The author notes that 'principles' can mean what the art is built upon, or the premises of proofs. Identify both.
This distinction is key to understanding the debate.
3
Analyze the objection
The passage presents the well-known view that principles are what the issue depends on, while premises are for commencement. Conception of subject is a premise, so it cannot be a principle.
This objection challenges Ibn Sina's inclusion.
4
Consider the counter-argument
The author suggests that 'principles' here might mean both essence and commencement, but finds this unsatisfactory. Then he addresses the linguistic vs. technical sense of Ibn Sina's statement.
The debate remains open.

قوله: (فإنه) أن الموضوع أي موضوع العلم لا مفهوم الموضوع مطلقاً فإنه يبين في صناعة البرهان من المنطق أن لا اختصاص له بشيء من العلوم فيناسب إيراده في المنطق الذي هو آلة لجميع العلوم. قوله: (من المبادي ويعلم من كلاميه أنه قد يطلق المبادي على ما يبتني عليه الصناعة مطلقاً، وقد يطلق على المقدمات التي يبرهن منها على تلك الصناعة، فيمكن أن يكون عد إنية موضوعة جزءاً ثالثاً نظراً إلى المعنى الثاني الذي His saying: (for it) that the subject, i.e., the subject of the science, not the concept of the subject absolutely, for it is explained in the art of demonstration from logic that it does not belong specifically to any of the sciences, so it is appropriate to mention it in logic which is the tool for all sciences. His saying: (from the conceptual principles) because it serves as the subject of the issue; and what was said that it is a premise for the premise of the commencement because the assent to its subject-ness depends on it, so how is it counted among the principles? So in it (the reply) that its being from the principles of commencement does not negate its being from the principles of the sciences. It was said: the subject itself, even if it is among the conceptual principles... (continues)... his saying: (and its thatness) i.e., its existence, from the assertive principles called among them 'postulated principles' as Ibn Sina stated in his Burhan al-Shifa'. (They) i.e., issues, except that he counted it as a separate sought thing due to the intense connection of issues to it; and in it that it contradicts what they said in their argument that what does not know its existence, how can one seek a thing for it? For it is clear that what is meant is its existence. His saying: (outside of it) by agreement, and that is because the assent to the subject-ness of the subject after it becomes a subject, and it is after the investigation of its essential accidents, so how can it be part of the science? His saying: (I mean its existence from the assertive principles etc.) i.e., if its existence is hidden, he clarified it in al-Shifa where he said: 'The subject of an art must be assented to and conceived together. And what is hidden in both existence and definition, like number, the one, and the point, they posit its existence as well. And positing its existence is among the principles of the art called 'postulated principles' because it is a doubted premise upon which the art is built.' End. He also said in a preceding chapter: 'For each of the arts, especially the theoretical, there are principles, subjects, and issues. The principles are the premises from which proofs are derived for that art.' He also said: 'The specific principles of the issues of a science are of two divisions: either specific to the whole science or to an issue or issues.' End. And from his two statements it is known that 'principles' may be applied to what the art is built upon absolutely, and may be applied to the premises from which proofs are derived for that art. So it is possible that counting the thatness of the subject as a third part is with respect to the second meaning, by which the principles are made a part of the science, even if it falls under the principles in the first meaning. And to this refers the statement of al-Allama al-Taftazani in Sharh al-Maqasid. And in the description of the commentator (may God have mercy on him) of the assertive principles by saying 'which are called postulated principles' is a refutation of the statement that the Shaykh counted it among the assertive principles in the linguistic sense, not the technical sense. As a separate part, there remains here a discussion: the well-known among them is that the principles are what the very issue depends on, and the premise is what the commencement depends on. The conception of the subject was something upon which the assent to its subject-ness depends, and it is from the premises of commencement by agreement; its conception was a premise of the premise, so how can it be counted among the principles? Unless it is said that what is meant by principles here is what the issue depends on in terms of essence and commencement, not the technical principles, and there is something in it. His saying: (and its thatness) i.e., its existence, from the assertive principles. It may be said: The technical assertive principles according to them are the premises that constitute the syllogisms of the science, and the assent to the existence of the subject is not from them. And it is objected to him that some assertive principles, the validity of the proof depends on them, and they are not composed from them; and then the parts of the sciences are not confined to three. Yes, in counting the thatness of the subject as a postulated principle, there is consideration because they are the propositions that constitute...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed critical discussion about the status of the subject (الموضوع) of a science and its existence (الإنية) in relation to the principles of that science. The author begins by examining the nature of the subject itself: it is the subject of the science, not the absolute concept of subject, which is discussed in logic. The author notes that this is particularly for subjects whose existence is not obvious. Counting the existence as a third part (subject, principles, issues) may be based on the second meaning. The passage includes further debate: the well-known view distinguishes between principles (what the issue itself depends on) and premises (what commencement depends on). How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina and later scholars debated whether the existence of the subject is a principle or merely a premise for commencement. In your own life, what foundational belief do you hold that you have never examined—is it a principle you assume or a premise you arrived at through reasoning? Will you take time this week to trace its foundation?
?
The commentator refuted the claim that Ibn Sina used 'assertive principles' in the technical sense, showing that even great scholars can be misunderstood. Are there any beliefs about Islam that you hold based on a misunderstanding of what scholars said? What would it cost you to investigate them?
?
The passage distinguishes between what a science depends on essentially versus for commencement. In your faith, do you treat some doctrines as essential (principles) and others as merely practical (premises)? How confident are you that you have identified the truly essential ones?

The Existence of the Subject as a Tasdiqi Principle
The commentator discusses whether the subject's existence is a tasdiqi principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of the subject of a science is considered among the tasdiqi principles and how Ibn Sina's classification is interpreted by the commentator.

Core Idea
The subject's existence is a principle because the science's problems depend on it.

This lesson explores a subtle but important question: Should the mere existence of the subject of a science be counted among its principles? The commentator engages with Ibn Sina's classification and clarifies how different meanings of 'principles' lead to different conclusions. By the end, you will see why this matter is not merely terminological but affects the structure of a science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main question
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that the core issue is whether the existence of the subject (anniyya) is a tasdiqi principle or not.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أنيته أعني وجوده من المبادي التصديقية'.
2
Follow Ibn Sina's distinction
In the cited passage from Burhan al-Shifa, Ibn Sina distinguishes between subjects with manifest existence (like natural body) and those with hidden existence (like number). Observe how this classification determines whether existence is included as a principle.
Ibn Sina says that for subjects with hidden existence, their existence must be placed among the principles.
3
Examine the objections and responses
The commentator presents an objection: if the subject's existence is not known, how can one seek its accidents? He also addresses the objection that tasdiqi principles are only premises of syllogisms. See how he defends Ibn Sina's view.
The objection is based on the technical meaning of 'tasdiqi principles'.
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider how this discussion relates to the definition of principles and the structure of a science. The commentator suggests that 'principles' can be understood in two ways: what the science is built upon, or premises of demonstration.
This ambiguity explains why Ibn Sina's inclusion of existence might be seen as part of the science or not.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : مسائله التي هي المقاصد
الشروع فيه الخارجة عنه اتفاقا وأنيته أعني وجوده من المبادي التصديقية المسماة عندهم أصولاً موضوعة كما صرح به ابن سينا في برهان الشفاء ( وهي ) أي مسائل إلا أنه عد مطلوباً برأسه لشدة ارتباط المسائل به على ما يبتني عليه الصناعة مطلقاً، وقد يطلق على المقدمات التي يبرهن منها على تلك الصناعة، فيمكن أن يكون عد إنية موضوعه جزءاً ثالثاً نظراً إلى المعنى الثاني الذي باعتباره جعل المبادي جزءاً من العلم، وإن المبادي التصديقية بالمعنى اللغوي لا بالمعنى المصطلح . جزءاً برأسه بقي ها هنا بحث وهو أن المشهور فيما بينهم هو أن المبادي ما يتوقف عليه ذات المسألة، والمقدمة ما يتوقف عليه الشروع وتصور الموضوع ما كان المصطلح عليها وفيه ما فيه . قوله : ( وأنيته) أعني وجوده من المبادي التصديقية قد يقال : المبادي التصديقية المصطلح عليها عندهم هي المقدمات التي يتألف منها قياسات العلم، والتصديق بوجود الموضوع ليس منها، The First Observatory - The Fifth Purpose: Its problems which are the purposes. The commencement in it is external to it, unanimously, and its thatness, meaning its existence, is among the tasdiqi principles called by them postulated principles, as Ibn Sina stated in the Burhan of al-Shifa. (And they) meaning problems, except that he considered it a sought-after thing by itself due to the strong connection of the problems to it. And in it is that it contradicts what they said in their explanation: 'How is the establishment of something for it sought when its existence is not known?' For it is explicit that what is meant by it is its existence. His statement: (External to it) unanimously. That is because the affirmation of the subjectivity of the subject after it becomes a subject is after the investigation of its essential accidents, so how can it be a part of the science? His statement: (I mean its existence from the tasdiqi principles, etc.) i.e., if its existence is hidden, he stated it in al-Shifa where he said: 'The subject of a craft must be affirmed and conceived together. So what has manifest existence but hidden definition, like natural body, its existence is not placed in the science, but rather one busies himself with placing only its definition. And what has both hidden existence and definition, like number, one, and point, they also place its existence. And placing its existence is among the principles of the craft that are called postulated principles, because it is a premise that is doubted and upon which the craft is built.' End. It remains that he said in a preceding chapter to this chapter that each craft, especially the theoretical, has principles, subjects, and problems. The principles are the premises from which one demonstrates for that craft. He also said in it: 'The specific principles for the problems of a science are of two kinds: either specific to that whole science, or specific to a problem or problems.' End. And it is known from his two statements that 'principles' may be applied to what the craft is built upon absolutely, and may be applied to the premises from which one demonstrates for that craft. So it is possible that counting the thatness of its subject as a third part is with regard to the second meaning, by which the principles were made a part of the science, even if it is included in the principles in the first meaning. And to this refers the statement of al-Allama al-Taftazani in Sharh al-Maqasid. And in the commentator's description, may God have mercy on him, of the tasdiqi principles with his statement 'which are called postulated principles' is a refutation of the claim that the master counted it among the tasdiqi principles in the linguistic sense, not in the terminological sense. As an independent part, there remains a discussion here: the well-known among them is that the principles are that upon which the essence of the problem depends, and the premise is that upon which the commencement depends. The conception of the subject is that upon which the affirmation of its subjectivity depends, and it is among the premises of commencement unanimously. If its conception is the premise of the premise, how can it be counted among the principles? Unless it is said: what is meant by principles here is that upon which the problem depends essentially and in commencement, not the terminological principles. And in it is what is in it. His statement: (And its thatness) I mean its existence from the tasdiqi principles. It may be said: the terminological tasdiqi principles according to them are the premises that compose the syllogisms of the science, and the affirmation of the existence of the subject is not among them. As for Ibn Sina's explicit statement that the affirmation of existence is among the tasdiqi principles, he meant it in the linguistic sense, in that the proof of the essential accidents of the subject depends on it. And it is objected to him that some tasdiqi principles depend on the validity of the proof, and it does not compose it, and then the parts of the sciences are not limited to three. Indeed, in counting the existence of the subject among the postulated principles there is reflection, because they are the premises that compose...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion about whether the existence (anniyya) of the subject of a science should be considered among the tasdiqi (affirmative) principles. The commentator begins by noting that the existence of the subject is unanimously considered external to the science itself, yet Ibn Sina included it among the tasdiqi principles called 'postulated principles' (usul mawdu'a). He then explains that Ibn Sina counted it as a sought-after thing by itself due to the strong connection of the problems to the subject. An objection is raised: if the subject's existence is not known, how can one seek to establish something for it? The response is that the term 'existence' here refers to the subject's existence. The commentator then cites Ibn Sina's discussion from Burhan al-Shifa, where he distinguishes between things with manifest existence and hidden definition (like natural body) and things with both hidden existence and definition (like number and point). Ibn Sina stated that for the latter, the existence must be placed among the principles. The commentator further analyzes Ibn Sina's two statements on principles, showing that 'principles' can be used broadly for what the science is built upon, or specifically for premises from which demonstration proceeds. He suggests that counting the subject's thatness as a third part of the science is based on the second meaning. He also notes that al-Taftazani's description refutes the claim that Ibn Sina meant only the linguistic sense. A later discussion addresses whether the existence of the subject is truly a principle, given that principles are what the essence of the problem depends on, while the conception of the subject is a premise for the commencement. The passage ends with the objection that tasdiqi principles in the technical sense are premises of syllogisms, not affirmations of existence, and that Ibn Sina likely meant the linguistic sense. The commentator finds this objection insufficient, noting that some tasdiqi principles are needed for the validity of the proof even if they do not compose it, and that the sciences would then not be limited to three parts. He concludes with a reflective remark about considering the subject's existence among the postulated principles.

Key Terms
المبادي التصديقية
أنيته
الأصول الموضوعة
برهان الشفاء
المبادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ibn Sina's inclusion of the subject's existence among the principles is purely linguistic and not terminological.
Correction: The commentator shows that it can also be understood terminologically when 'principles' is taken in the second sense (premises of demonstration), as al-Taftazani's comment suggests.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does Ibn Sina include the existence of the subject among the principles?
A) Because the subject's existence is always unknown
B) Because the science's problems depend on the subject's existence
C) Because Ibn Sina meant it only in the linguistic sense
D) Because the subject's existence is a premise of commencement

Key Takeaway
The existence of the subject is a tasdiqi principle in the sense that it is a necessary premise for the science, even if it is not a premise in every syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge about a subject—like the existence of God or the purpose of life—do you first affirm its existence with certainty, or do you assume it and then investigate? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the foundations of your own beliefs?
?
The passage says the subject's existence is a 'doubting premise' that the science is built upon. Is there any belief you hold that you have not truly examined? Will you commit to questioning one such assumption this week, no matter how unsettling?
?
Ibn Sina distinguishes between subjects whose existence is manifest and those hidden. Are there areas in your life where you are content with 'manifest existence' without deeper investigation? What would change if you treated them as hidden and sought true affirmation?
